<div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ecologically_grown_vegetables.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Ecologically_grown_vegetables.jpg/330px-Ecologically_grown_vegetables.jpg" width="330" height="248" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Ecologically_grown_vegetables.jpg/495px-Ecologically_grown_vegetables.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Ecologically_grown_vegetables.jpg/660px-Ecologically_grown_vegetables.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ecologically_grown_vegetables.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Vegetables" class="mw-redirect" title="Vegetables">Vegetables</a> from ecological farming.</div>

<p><b>Organic farming</b> is an alternative agricultural system which originated early in the 20th century in reaction to rapidly changing farming practices. Organic agriculture continues to be developed by various organic agriculture organizations today. It relies on fertilizers of organic origin such as <a href="/wiki/Compost" title="Compost">compost</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Green_manure" title="Green manure">green manure</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bone_meal" title="Bone meal">bone meal</a> and places emphasis on techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Companion_planting" title="Companion planting">companion planting</a>. <a href="/wiki/Biological_pest_control" title="Biological pest control">Biological pest control</a>, mixed cropping and the fostering of insect predators are encouraged. In general, organic standards are designed to allow the use of naturally occurring substances while prohibiting or strictly limiting synthetic substances.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> For instance, naturally occurring pesticides such as <a href="/wiki/Pyrethrin" title="Pyrethrin">pyrethrin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rotenone" title="Rotenone">rotenone</a> are permitted, while synthetic <a href="/wiki/Fertilizers" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertilizers">fertilizers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> are generally prohibited. Synthetic substances that are allowed include, for example, <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_sulfate" title="Copper(II) sulfate">copper sulfate</a>, elemental <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ivermectin" title="Ivermectin">Ivermectin</a>. <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">Genetically modified organisms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanomaterials" title="Nanomaterials">nanomaterials</a>, human sewage <a href="/wiki/Sludge" title="Sludge">sludge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plant_growth_regulator" class="mw-redirect" title="Plant growth regulator">plant growth regulators</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic_use_in_livestock" title="Antibiotic use in livestock">antibiotic use in livestock</a> husbandry are prohibited.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Reasons for advocation of organic farming include real or perceived advantages in <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-isbn_9780930031756_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isbn_9780930031756-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Openness" title="Openness">openness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Self-sufficiency" title="Self-sufficiency">self-sufficiency</a>, <a href="/wiki/Autonomy" title="Autonomy">autonomy</a>/<a href="/wiki/Independence" title="Independence">independence</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-isbn_9780930031756_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isbn_9780930031756-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Health" title="Health">health</a>, <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Food_safety" title="Food safety">food safety</a>, although the match between perception and reality is continually challenged.</p>
<p>Organic agricultural methods are internationally regulated and legally enforced by many nations, based in large part on the standards set by the <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_of_Organic_Agriculture_Movements" class="mw-redirect" title="International Federation of Organic Agriculture Movements">International Federation of Organic Agriculture Movements</a> (IFOAM), an international <a href="/wiki/Umbrella_organization" title="Umbrella organization">umbrella organization</a> for organic farming organizations established in 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Organic agriculture can be defined as:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>an integrated farming system that strives for sustainability, the enhancement of soil fertility and biological diversity whilst, with rare exceptions, prohibiting synthetic pesticides, antibiotics, synthetic fertilizers, genetically modified organisms, and growth hormones.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Since 1990 the market for <a href="/wiki/Organic_food" title="Organic food">organic food</a> and other products has grown rapidly, reaching $63 billion worldwide in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-OrgWorldYearbook2013_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OrgWorldYearbook2013-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:25</sup> This demand has driven a similar increase in organically managed farmland that grew from 2001 to 2011 at a compounding rate of 8.9% per annum.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> As of 2011, approximately 37,000,000 hectares (91,000,000 acres) worldwide were farmed organically, representing approximately 0.9 percent of total world farmland.<sup id="cite_ref-OrgWorldYearbook2013_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OrgWorldYearbook2013-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:1</sup></p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Terminology"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Terminology</span></a></li>
</ul>
</li>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_organic_farming" title="History of organic farming">History of organic farming</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Traditional_agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditional agriculture">Agriculture</a> was practiced for thousands of years without the use of artificial chemicals. <a href="/wiki/Artificial_fertilizer" class="mw-redirect" title="Artificial fertilizer">Artificial fertilizers</a> were first created during the mid-19th century. These <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer#History" title="Fertilizer">early fertilizers</a> were cheap, powerful, and easy to transport in bulk. Similar advances occurred in chemical <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> in the 1940s, leading to the decade being referred to as the 'pesticide era'.<sup id="cite_ref-Horne2_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horne2-14">[14]</a></sup> These new agricultural techniques, while beneficial in the short term, had serious longer term side effects such as <a href="/wiki/Soil_compaction" title="Soil compaction">soil compaction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">erosion</a>, and declines in overall <a href="/wiki/Soil_fertility" title="Soil fertility">soil fertility</a>, along with health concerns about toxic chemicals entering the food supply.<sup id="cite_ref-Stinner2007_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stinner2007-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:10</sup> In the late 1800s and early 1900s, soil biology scientists began to seek ways to remedy these side effects while still maintaining higher production.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Biodynamic_agriculture" title="Biodynamic agriculture">Biodynamic agriculture</a> was the first modern system of agriculture to focus exclusively on organic methods.<sup id="cite_ref-PaullJ_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PaullJ-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vogt_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vogt-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kirchmann_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirchmann-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:</sup> Its development began in 1924 with a series of eight lectures on agriculture given by <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Steiner" title="Rudolf Steiner">Rudolf Steiner</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> These lectures, the first known presentation of what later came to be known as <a href="/wiki/Organic_agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="Organic agriculture">organic agriculture</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-PaullJ_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PaullJ-16">[16]</a></sup> were held in response to a request by farmers who noticed degraded soil conditions and a deterioration in the health and quality of crops and livestock resulting from the use of chemical fertilizers.<sup id="cite_ref-Introduction_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Introduction-22">[22]</a></sup> The one hundred eleven attendees, less than half of whom were farmers, came from six countries, primarily Germany and Poland.<sup id="cite_ref-PaullJ_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PaullJ-16">[16]</a></sup> The lectures were published in November 1924; the first English translation appeared in 1928 as <i>The Agriculture Course</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1921, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Albert_Howard" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Albert Howard">Albert Howard</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Gabrielle_Howard" title="Gabrielle Howard">Gabrielle Howard</a>, accomplished <a href="/wiki/Botanist" class="mw-redirect" title="Botanist">botanists</a>, founded an Institute of Plant Industry to improve traditional farming methods in India. Among other things, they brought improved implements and improved animal husbandry methods from their scientific training; then by incorporating aspects of the local traditional methods, developed protocalls for the rotation of crops, erosion prevention techniques, and the systematic use of composts and manures.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Stimulated by these experiences of traditional farming, when Albert Howard returned to Britain in the early 1930s<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> he began to promulgate a system of natural agriculture.</p>
<p>In July 1939, <a href="/wiki/Ehrenfried_Pfeiffer" title="Ehrenfried Pfeiffer">Ehrenfried Pfeiffer</a>, the author of the standard work on biodynamic agriculture (<i>Bio-Dynamic Farming and Gardening</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Paull2011_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paull2011-26">[26]</a></sup> came to the UK at the invitation of <a href="/wiki/Walter_James,_4th_Baron_Northbourne" title="Walter James, 4th Baron Northbourne">Walter James, 4th Baron Northbourne</a> as a presenter at the <a href="/wiki/Betteshanger" title="Betteshanger">Betteshanger</a> Summer School and Conference on Biodynamic Farming at Northbourne's farm in Kent.<sup id="cite_ref-Betteshanger_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Betteshanger-27">[27]</a></sup> One of the chief purposes of the conference was to bring together the proponents of various approaches to organic agriculture in order that they might cooperate within a larger movement. Howard attended the conference, where he met Pfeiffer.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In the following year, Northbourne published his manifesto of organic farming, <i>Look to the Land</i>, in which he coined the term "organic farming." The Betteshanger conference has been described as the 'missing link' between biodynamic agriculture and other forms of organic farming.<sup id="cite_ref-Betteshanger_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Betteshanger-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1940 Howard published his <i>An Agricultural Testament</i>. In this book he adopted Northbourne's terminology of "organic farming."<sup id="cite_ref-Paull_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paull-29">[29]</a></sup> Howard's work spread widely, and he became known as the "father of organic farming" for his work in applying scientific knowledge and principles to various traditional and natural methods.<sup id="cite_ref-Stinner2007_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stinner2007-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:45</sup> In the United States <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Irving_Rodale" class="mw-redirect" title="Jerome Irving Rodale">J.I. Rodale</a>, who was keenly interested both in Howard's ideas and in biodynamics,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> founded in the 1940s both a working organic farm for trials and experimentation, <a href="/wiki/The_Rodale_Institute" title="The Rodale Institute">The Rodale Institute</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Rodale_Press" class="mw-redirect" title="Rodale Press">Rodale Press</a> to teach and advocate organic methods to the wider public. These became important influences on the spread of organic agriculture. Further work was done by <a href="/wiki/Lady_Eve_Balfour" title="Lady Eve Balfour">Lady Eve Balfour</a> in the United Kingdom, and many others across the world.</p>
<p>Increasing environmental awareness in the general population in modern times has transformed the originally supply-driven organic movement to a demand-driven one. Premium prices and some government subsidies attracted farmers. In the developing world, many producers farm according to traditional methods that are comparable to organic farming, but not certified, and that may not include the latest scientific advancements in organic agriculture. In other cases, farmers in the developing world have converted to modern organic methods for economic reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Biodynamic agriculturists, who based their work on Steiner's spiritually-oriented <a href="/wiki/Anthroposophy" title="Anthroposophy">anthroposophy</a>, used the term "organic" to indicate that a farm should be viewed as a living organism,<sup id="cite_ref-Kirchmann_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirchmann-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:17–19</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Betteshanger_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Betteshanger-27">[27]</a></sup> in the sense of the following quotation:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"An organic farm, properly speaking, is not one that uses certain methods and substances and avoids others; it is a farm whose structure is formed in imitation of the structure of a natural system that has the integrity, the independence and the benign dependence of an organism"</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Wendell_Berry" title="Wendell Berry">Wendell Berry</a>, "The Gift of Good Land"</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The use of "organic" popularized by Howard and Rodale, on the other hand, refers more narrowly to the use of <a href="/wiki/Soil_organic_matter" title="Soil organic matter">organic matter</a> derived from plant compost and animal manures to improve the <a href="/wiki/Humus" title="Humus">humus</a> content of soils, grounded in the work of early soil scientists who developed what was then called "humus farming." Since the early 1940s the two camps have tended to merge.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods">Methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Organic-vegetable-cultivation.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Organic-vegetable-cultivation.jpeg/220px-Organic-vegetable-cultivation.jpeg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Organic-vegetable-cultivation.jpeg/330px-Organic-vegetable-cultivation.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Organic-vegetable-cultivation.jpeg/440px-Organic-vegetable-cultivation.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="2848" data-file-height="2136" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Organic-vegetable-cultivation.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Organic cultivation of mixed vegetables in <a href="/wiki/Capay,_California" title="Capay, California">Capay, California</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"Organic agriculture is a production system that sustains the health of soils, <a href="/wiki/Ecosystems" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecosystems">ecosystems</a> and people. It relies on ecological processes, <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> and cycles adapted to local conditions, rather than the use of inputs with adverse effects. Organic agriculture combines tradition, innovation and science to benefit the shared environment and promote fair relationships and a good quality of life for all involved..."</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_of_Organic_Agriculture_Movements" class="mw-redirect" title="International Federation of Organic Agriculture Movements">International Federation of Organic Agriculture Movements</a><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Organic farming methods combine scientific knowledge of ecology and modern <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a> with <a href="/wiki/Traditional_agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditional agriculture">traditional farming</a> practices based on naturally occurring biological processes. Organic farming methods are studied in the field of <a href="/wiki/Agroecology" title="Agroecology">agroecology</a>. While conventional agriculture uses synthetic pesticides and water-soluble synthetically purified fertilizers, organic farmers are restricted by regulations to using natural pesticides and fertilizers. An example of a natural pesticide is <a href="/wiki/Pyrethrin" title="Pyrethrin">pyrethrin</a>, which is found naturally in the <a href="/wiki/Chrysanthemum" title="Chrysanthemum">Chrysanthemum</a> flower. The principal methods of organic farming include <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Green_manure" title="Green manure">green manures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Compost" title="Compost">compost</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biological_pest_control" title="Biological pest control">biological pest control</a>, and mechanical <a href="/wiki/Tillage" title="Tillage">cultivation</a>. These measures use the natural environment to enhance agricultural productivity: <a href="/wiki/Legumes" class="mw-redirect" title="Legumes">legumes</a> are planted to fix <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> into the soil, <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">natural insect predators</a> are encouraged, crops are rotated to confuse pests and renew soil, and natural materials such as <a href="/wiki/Potassium_bicarbonate" title="Potassium bicarbonate">potassium bicarbonate</a><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mulch" title="Mulch">mulches</a> are used to control disease and <a href="/wiki/Weed" title="Weed">weeds</a>. <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">Genetically modified</a> seeds and animals are excluded.</p>
<p>While organic is fundamentally different from conventional because of the use of carbon based fertilizers compared with highly soluble synthetic based fertilizers and <a href="/wiki/Biological_pest_control" title="Biological pest control">biological pest control</a> instead of synthetic pesticides, organic farming and large-scale conventional farming are not entirely mutually exclusive. Many of the methods developed for organic agriculture have been borrowed by more conventional agriculture. For example, <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Pest_Management" class="mw-redirect" title="Integrated Pest Management">Integrated Pest Management</a> is a multifaceted strategy that uses various organic methods of pest control whenever possible, but in conventional farming could include synthetic <a href="/wiki/Pesticides" class="mw-redirect" title="Pesticides">pesticides</a> only as a last resort.<sup id="cite_ref-EPA_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPA-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crop_diversity">Crop diversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Crop diversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Organic farming encourages <a href="/wiki/Crop_diversity" title="Crop diversity">Crop diversity</a>. The science of <a href="/wiki/Agroecology" title="Agroecology">agroecology</a> has revealed the benefits of <a href="/wiki/Polyculture" title="Polyculture">polyculture</a> (multiple crops in the same space), which is often employed in organic farming.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Planting a variety of vegetable crops supports a wider range of beneficial insects, soil microorganisms, and other factors that add up to overall farm health. Crop diversity helps environments thrive and protects species from going extinct.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soil_management">Soil management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Soil management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Organic farming relies heavily on the natural breakdown of organic matter, using techniques like <a href="/wiki/Green_manure" title="Green manure">green manure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Composting" class="mw-redirect" title="Composting">composting</a>, to replace nutrients taken from the soil by previous crops. This biological process, driven by <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Mycorrhiza" title="Mycorrhiza">mycorrhiza</a>, allows the natural production of nutrients in the soil throughout the growing season, and has been referred to as <i>feeding the soil to feed the plant.</i> Organic farming uses a variety of methods to improve soil fertility, including crop rotation, cover cropping, reduced tillage, and application of compost. By reducing tillage, soil is not inverted and exposed to air; less carbon is lost to the atmosphere resulting in more soil organic carbon. This has an added benefit of carbon sequestration, which can reduce green house gases and help reverse climate change.</p>
<p>Plants need <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Micronutrient" title="Micronutrient">micronutrients</a> and <a href="/wiki/Symbiotic_relationship" class="mw-redirect" title="Symbiotic relationship">symbiotic relationships</a> with <a href="/wiki/Fungi" class="mw-redirect" title="Fungi">fungi</a> and other organisms to flourish, but getting enough nitrogen, and particularly synchronization so that plants get enough nitrogen at the right time (when plants need it most), is a challenge for organic farmers.<sup id="cite_ref-SoilFertility_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SoilFertility-39">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">Crop rotation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Green_manure" title="Green manure">green manure</a> ("<a href="/wiki/Cover_crop" title="Cover crop">cover crops</a>") help to provide nitrogen through <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">legumes</a> (more precisely, the <i><a href="/wiki/Fabaceae" title="Fabaceae">Fabaceae</a></i> family), which fix nitrogen from the atmosphere through symbiosis with <a href="/wiki/Rhizobia" title="Rhizobia">rhizobial</a> <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>. <a href="/wiki/Intercropping" title="Intercropping">Intercropping</a>, which is sometimes used for insect and disease control, can also increase soil nutrients, but the competition between the legume and the crop can be problematic and wider spacing between crop rows is required. <a href="/wiki/Crop_residue" title="Crop residue">Crop residues</a> can be <a href="/wiki/Plough" title="Plough">ploughed</a> back into the soil, and different plants leave different amounts of nitrogen, potentially aiding synchronization.<sup id="cite_ref-SoilFertility_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SoilFertility-39">[39]</a></sup> Organic farmers also use animal <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a>, certain processed fertilizers such as seed meal and various <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">mineral</a> powders such as <a href="/wiki/Rock_phosphate" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock phosphate">rock phosphate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greensand_(geology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Greensand (geology)">green sand</a>, a naturally occurring form of <a href="/wiki/Potash" title="Potash">potash</a> that provides potassium. Together these methods help to control <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a>. In some cases <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> may need to be amended. Natural pH amendments include <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_lime" title="Agricultural lime">lime</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, but in the U.S. some compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_sulfate" title="Iron(II) sulfate">iron sulfate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminum_sulfate" class="mw-redirect" title="Aluminum sulfate">aluminum sulfate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_sulfate" title="Magnesium sulfate">magnesium sulfate</a>, and soluble <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a> products are allowed in organic farming.<sup id="cite_ref-Gillman2008_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gillman2008-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:43</sup></p>
<p>Mixed farms with both <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tillage" title="Tillage">crops</a> can operate as <a href="/wiki/Ley_farming" class="mw-redirect" title="Ley farming">ley farms</a>, whereby the land gathers fertility through growing nitrogen-fixing <a href="/wiki/Forage" title="Forage">forage</a> grasses such as <a href="/wiki/White_clover" class="mw-redirect" title="White clover">white clover</a> or <a href="/wiki/Alfalfa" title="Alfalfa">alfalfa</a> and grows <a href="/wiki/Cash_crop" title="Cash crop">cash crops</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereals</a> when fertility is established. Farms without livestock ("stockless") may find it more difficult to maintain soil fertility, and may rely more on external inputs such as imported <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a> as well as grain legumes and green manures, although grain legumes may fix limited nitrogen because they are harvested. <a href="/wiki/Horticulture" title="Horticulture">Horticultural</a> farms that grow fruits and vegetables in protected conditions often relay even more on external inputs.<sup id="cite_ref-SoilFertility_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SoilFertility-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Biological research into soil and soil organisms has proven beneficial to organic farming. Varieties of bacteria and fungi break down chemicals, plant matter and animal waste into productive soil nutrients. In turn, they produce benefits of healthier yields and more productive soil for future crops.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Fields with less or no manure display significantly lower yields, due to decreased soil microbe community. Increased manure improves biological activity, providing a healthier, more arable soil system and higher yields.<sup id="cite_ref-Flie.C3.9Fbach_et_al._2006_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flie.C3.9Fbach_et_al._2006-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weed_management">Weed management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Weed management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Organic <a href="/wiki/Weed" title="Weed">weed</a> management promotes weed suppression, rather than weed elimination, by enhancing crop competition and <a href="/wiki/Phytotoxic" class="mw-redirect" title="Phytotoxic">phytotoxic</a> effects on weeds.<sup id="cite_ref-extension.iastate.edu_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-extension.iastate.edu-43">[43]</a></sup> Organic farmers integrate cultural, biological, mechanical, physical and chemical tactics to manage weeds without synthetic herbicides.</p>
<p>Organic standards require <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">rotation</a> of annual crops,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> meaning that a single crop cannot be grown in the same location without a different, intervening crop. Organic crop rotations frequently include weed-suppressive <a href="/wiki/Cover_crop" title="Cover crop">cover crops</a> and crops with dissimilar life cycles to discourage weeds associated with a particular crop.<sup id="cite_ref-extension.iastate.edu_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-extension.iastate.edu-43">[43]</a></sup> Research is ongoing to develop organic methods to promote the growth of natural microorganisms that suppress the growth or germination of common weeds.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other cultural practices used to enhance crop competitiveness and reduce weed pressure include selection of competitive crop varieties, high-density planting, tight row spacing, and late planting into warm soil to encourage rapid crop <a href="/wiki/Germination" title="Germination">germination</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-extension.iastate.edu_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-extension.iastate.edu-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mechanical and physical weed control practices used on organic farms can be broadly grouped as:<sup id="cite_ref-extension.org_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-extension.org-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tillage" title="Tillage">Tillage</a> - Turning the soil between crops to incorporate crop residues and soil amendments; remove existing weed growth and prepare a seedbed for planting; turning soil after seeding to kill weeds, including <a href="/wiki/Cultivator" title="Cultivator">cultivation</a> of row crops;</li>
<li>Mowing and cutting - Removing top growth of weeds;</li>
<li>Flame weeding and thermal weeding - Using heat to kill weeds; and</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mulch" title="Mulch">Mulching</a> - Blocking weed emergence with organic materials, plastic films, or <a href="/wiki/Landscape_fabric" title="Landscape fabric">landscape fabric</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Some critics, citing work published in 1997 by David Pimentel of Cornell University,<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> which described an epidemic of <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">soil erosion</a> worldwide, have raised concerned that tillage contribute to the erosion epidemic.<sup id="cite_ref-Economist_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-49">[49]</a></sup> The FAO and other organizations have advocated a 'no-till' approach to both conventional and organic farming, and point out in particular that crop rotation techniques used in organic farming are excellent no-till approaches.<sup id="cite_ref-Economist_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> A study published in 2005 by Pimentel and colleagues<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> confirmed that 'Crop rotations and cover cropping (green manure) typical of organic agriculture reduce soil erosion, pest problems, and pesticide use.' Some naturally sourced chemicals are allowed for herbicidal use. These include certain formulations of <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a> (concentrated vinegar), <a href="/wiki/Corn_gluten_meal" title="Corn gluten meal">corn gluten meal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Essential_oils" class="mw-redirect" title="Essential oils">essential oils</a>. A few selective <a href="/wiki/Bioherbicide" title="Bioherbicide">bioherbicides</a> based on fungal <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a> have also been developed. At this time, however, organic herbicides and bioherbicides play a minor role in the organic weed control toolbox.<sup id="cite_ref-extension.org_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-extension.org-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Weeds can be controlled by grazing. For example, geese have been used successfully to weed a range of organic crops including cotton, strawberries, tobacco, and corn,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> reviving the practice of keeping <a href="/wiki/Cotton_Patch_Goose" class="mw-redirect" title="Cotton Patch Goose">cotton patch geese</a>, common in the southern U.S. before the 1950s. Similarly, some rice farmers introduce ducks and fish to wet <a href="/wiki/Paddy_field" title="Paddy field">paddy fields</a> to eat both weeds and insects.<sup id="cite_ref-csmonitor.com_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-csmonitor.com-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controlling_other_organisms">Controlling other organisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Controlling other organisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG/220px-Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG/330px-Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG/440px-Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Chloroxylon" title="Chloroxylon">Chloroxylon</a> is used for Pest Management in Organic Rice Cultivation in Chhattisgarh, India</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Biological_pest_control" title="Biological pest control">Biological pest control</a> and <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Pest_Management" class="mw-redirect" title="Integrated Pest Management">Integrated Pest Management</a></div>
<p>Organisms aside from weeds that cause problems on organic farms include <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a> (e.g., insects, <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a>), <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematodes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>. Organic practices include, but are not limited to:</p>
<ul>
<li>encouraging predatory beneficial insects to control pests by serving them nursery plants and/or an alternative habitat, usually in a form of a <a href="/wiki/Windbreak" title="Windbreak">shelterbelt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hedgerow" class="mw-redirect" title="Hedgerow">hedgerow</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Beetle_bank" title="Beetle bank">beetle bank</a>;</li>
<li>encouraging beneficial microorganisms;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">rotating crops</a> to different locations from year to year to interrupt pest reproduction cycles;</li>
<li>planting <a href="/wiki/Companion_planting" title="Companion planting">companion crops</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_pest-repelling_plants" title="List of pest-repelling plants">pest-repelling plants</a> that discourage or divert pests;</li>
<li>using row covers to protect crops during pest migration periods;</li>
<li>using <a href="/wiki/Ecologic_pesticides_and_herbicides" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecologic pesticides and herbicides">biologic pesticides and herbicides</a>;</li>
<li>using <a href="/wiki/Stale_seed_bed" title="Stale seed bed">stale seed beds</a> to germinate and destroy weeds before planting;<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li>using sanitation to remove pest habitat;</li>
<li>using <a href="/wiki/Insect_trap" title="Insect trap">insect traps</a> to monitor and control insect populations; and</li>
<li>using physical barriers, such as <a href="/wiki/Row_cover" title="Row cover">row covers</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>Examples of predatory beneficial insects include <a href="/wiki/Orius_(bug)" class="mw-redirect" title="Orius (bug)">minute pirate bugs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geocoris" title="Geocoris">big-eyed bugs</a>, and to a lesser extent <a href="/wiki/Coccinellidae" title="Coccinellidae">ladybugs</a> (which tend to fly away), all of which eat a wide range of pests. <a href="/wiki/Neuroptera" title="Neuroptera">Lacewings</a> are also effective, but tend to fly away. <a href="/wiki/Praying_mantis" class="mw-redirect" title="Praying mantis">Praying mantis</a> tend to move more slowly and eat less heavily. <a href="/wiki/Parasitoid_wasp" title="Parasitoid wasp">Parasitoid wasps</a> tend to be effective for their selected prey, but like all small insects can be less effective outdoors because the wind controls their movement. Predatory mites are effective for controlling other mites.<sup id="cite_ref-Gillman2008_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gillman2008-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:66–90</sup></p>
<p>Naturally derived <a href="/wiki/Insecticide" title="Insecticide">insecticides</a> allowed for use on organic farms use include <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_thuringiensis" title="Bacillus thuringiensis">Bacillus thuringiensis</a></i> (a bacterial toxin), <a href="/wiki/Pyrethrum" title="Pyrethrum">pyrethrum</a> (a chrysanthemum extract), <a href="/wiki/Spinosad" title="Spinosad">spinosad</a> (a bacterial metabolite), <a href="/wiki/Neem" class="mw-redirect" title="Neem">neem</a> (a tree extract) and <a href="/wiki/Rotenone" title="Rotenone">rotenone</a> (a legume root extract). Fewer than 10% of organic farmers use these pesticides regularly; one survey found that only 5.3% of vegetable growers in California use <a href="/wiki/Rotenone" title="Rotenone">rotenone</a> while 1.7% use <a href="/wiki/Pyrethrum" title="Pyrethrum">pyrethrum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lotter2003_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lotter2003-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:26</sup> These pesticides are not always more safe or environmentally friendly than synthetic pesticides and can cause harm.<sup id="cite_ref-Gillman2008_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gillman2008-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:92</sup> The main criterion for organic pesticides is that they are naturally derived, and some naturally derived substances have been controversial. Controversial natural pesticides include <a href="/wiki/Rotenone" title="Rotenone">rotenone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicotine_sulfate" class="mw-redirect" title="Nicotine sulfate">nicotine sulfate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pyrethrum" title="Pyrethrum">pyrethrums</a><sup id="cite_ref-Misconcept_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Misconcept-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rotenone" title="Rotenone">Rotenone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyrethrum" title="Pyrethrum">pyrethrum</a> are particularly controversial because they work by attacking the nervous system, like most conventional insecticides. Rotenone is extremely toxic to fish<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> and can induce symptoms resembling Parkinson's disease in mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Although pyrethrum (natural pyrethrins) is more effective against insects when used with piperonyl butoxide (which retards degradation of the pyrethrins),<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> organic standards generally do not permit use of the latter substance.<sup id="cite_ref-CGSB2006b_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CGSB2006b-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OGA2004_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OGA2004-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7CFR205_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7CFR205-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Naturally derived <a href="/wiki/Fungicide" title="Fungicide">fungicides</a> allowed for use on organic farms include the bacteria <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_subtilis" title="Bacillus subtilis">Bacillus subtilis</a></i> and <i>Bacillus pumilus</i>; and the fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Trichoderma_harzianum" title="Trichoderma harzianum">Trichoderma harzianum</a></i>. These are mainly effective for diseases affecting roots. <a href="/wiki/Compost_tea" class="mw-redirect" title="Compost tea">Compost tea</a> contains a mix of beneficial microbes, which may attack or out-compete certain plant pathogens,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> but variability among formulations and preparation methods may contribute to inconsistent results or even dangerous growth of toxic microbes in compost teas.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some naturally derived pesticides are not allowed for use on organic farms. These include <a href="/wiki/Nicotine_sulfate" class="mw-redirect" title="Nicotine sulfate">nicotine sulfate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Strychnine" title="Strychnine">strychnine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-USDAlist_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USDAlist-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Synthetic pesticides allowed for use on organic farms include <a href="/wiki/Insecticidal_soap" title="Insecticidal soap">insecticidal soaps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horticultural_oil" title="Horticultural oil">horticultural oils</a> for insect management; and <a href="/wiki/Bordeaux_mixture" title="Bordeaux mixture">Bordeaux mixture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper_hydroxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Copper hydroxide">copper hydroxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_bicarbonate" title="Sodium bicarbonate">sodium bicarbonate</a> for managing fungi.<sup id="cite_ref-USDAlist_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USDAlist-67">[67]</a></sup> Copper sulfate and Bordeaux mixture (copper sulfate plus lime), approved for organic use in various jurisdictions,<sup id="cite_ref-CGSB2006b_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CGSB2006b-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OGA2004_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OGA2004-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USDAlist_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USDAlist-67">[67]</a></sup> can be more environmentally problematic than some synthetic fungicides dissallowed in organic farming<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Similar concerns apply to copper hydroxide. Repeated application of copper sulfate or copper hydroxide as a fungicide may eventually result in copper accumulation to toxic levels in soil,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> and admonitions to avoid excessive accumulations of copper in soil appear in various organic standards and elsewhere. Environmental concerns for several kinds of biota arise at average rates of use of such substances for some crops.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> In the European Union, where replacement of copper-based fungicides in organic agriculture is a policy priority,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> research is seeking alternatives for organic production.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Livestock">Livestock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Livestock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Organic_cattle_in_Ohio,_United_States.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Organic_cattle_in_Ohio%2C_United_States.jpg/220px-Organic_cattle_in_Ohio%2C_United_States.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Organic_cattle_in_Ohio%2C_United_States.jpg/330px-Organic_cattle_in_Ohio%2C_United_States.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Organic_cattle_in_Ohio%2C_United_States.jpg/440px-Organic_cattle_in_Ohio%2C_United_States.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="680" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Organic_cattle_in_Ohio,_United_States.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
For livestock like these healthy cows vaccines play an important part in animal health since antibiotic therapy is prohibited in organic farming</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Raising livestock and poultry, for meat, dairy and eggs, is another traditional farming activity that complements growing. Organic farms attempt to provide animals with natural living conditions and feed. Organic certification verifies that livestock are raised according to the USDA organic regulations throughout their lives.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> These regulations include the requirement that all animal feed must be certified organic.</p>
<p>Organic livestock may be, and must be, treated with medicine when they are sick, but drugs cannot be used to promote growth, their feed must be organic, and they must be pastured.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:19ff</sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also, horses and cattle were once a basic farm feature that provided labor, for hauling and plowing, fertility, through recycling of manure, and fuel, in the form of food for farmers and other animals. While today, small growing operations often do not include livestock, domesticated animals are a desirable part of the organic farming equation, especially for true sustainability, the ability of a farm to function as a self-renewing unit.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_modification">Genetic modification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Genetic modification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_crops" title="Genetically modified crops">Genetically modified crops</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food" title="Genetically modified food">Genetically modified food</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food_controversies" title="Genetically modified food controversies">Genetically modified food controversies</a></div>
<p>A key characteristic of organic farming is the rejection of genetically engineered plants and animals. On 19 October 1998, participants at IFOAM's 12th Scientific Conference issued the <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_of_Organic_Agriculture_Movements#IFOAM_and_GMOs" class="mw-redirect" title="International Federation of Organic Agriculture Movements">Mar del Plata Declaration</a>, where more than 600 delegates from over 60 countries voted unanimously to exclude the use of genetically modified organisms in food production and agriculture.</p>
<p>Although opposition to the use of any transgenic technologies in organic farming is strong, agricultural researchers Luis Herrera-Estrella and Ariel Alvarez-Morales continue to advocate integration of <a href="/wiki/Transgenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Transgenic">transgenic</a> technologies into organic farming as the optimal means to sustainable agriculture, particularly in the developing world,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> as does author and scientist <a href="/wiki/Pamela_Ronald" title="Pamela Ronald">Pamela Ronald</a>, who views this kind of <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> as being consistent with organic principles.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although GMOs are excluded from organic farming, there is concern that the pollen from genetically modified crops is increasingly penetrating organic and <a href="/wiki/Heirloom_plant" title="Heirloom plant">heirloom seed stocks</a>, making it difficult, if not impossible, to keep these genomes from entering the organic food supply. Differing regulations among countries limits the availability of GMOs to certain countries, as described in the article on <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_the_release_of_genetic_modified_organisms" class="mw-redirect" title="Regulation of the release of genetic modified organisms">regulation of the release of genetic modified organisms</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tools">Tools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Tools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Organic farmers use a number of traditional <a href="/wiki/Farm_tools" class="mw-redirect" title="Farm tools">farm tools</a> to do farming. Due to the goals of <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a> in organic farming, organic farmers try to minimize their reliance on <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>. In the developing world on small organic farms tools are normally constrained to hand tools and <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a> powered water pumps. A study evaluated the use of <a href="/wiki/Open-source" class="mw-redirect" title="Open-source">open-source</a> <a href="/wiki/3-D_printers" class="mw-redirect" title="3-D printers">3-D printers</a> (called <a href="/wiki/RepRaps" class="mw-redirect" title="RepRaps">RepRaps</a> using a bioplastic <a href="/wiki/Polylactic_acid" title="Polylactic acid">polylactic acid</a> (PLA) on organic farms.<sup id="cite_ref-organic3dp_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-organic3dp-79">[79]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Polylactic_acid" title="Polylactic acid">PLA</a> is a strong <a href="/wiki/Biodegradable" class="mw-redirect" title="Biodegradable">biodegradable</a> and <a href="/wiki/Recyclable" class="mw-redirect" title="Recyclable">recyclable</a> <a href="/wiki/Thermoplastic" title="Thermoplastic">thermoplastic</a> appropriate for a range of representative products in five categories of prints: <a href="/wiki/Handtools" class="mw-redirect" title="Handtools">handtools</a>, <a href="/wiki/Food_processing" title="Food processing">food processing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Animal_management" class="mw-redirect" title="Animal management">animal management</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_management" class="mw-redirect" title="Water management">water management</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydroponics" title="Hydroponics">hydroponics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-organic3dp_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-organic3dp-79">[79]</a></sup> Such <a href="/wiki/Open_source_hardware" class="mw-redirect" title="Open source hardware">open source hardware</a> is attractive to all types of small farmers as it provides control for farmers over their own equipment; this is exemplified by <a href="/wiki/Open_Source_Ecology" title="Open Source Ecology">Open Source Ecology</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Farm_Hack&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Farm Hack (page does not exist)">Farm Hack</a> and <a href="/wiki/FarmBot" title="FarmBot">FarmBot</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Standards">Standards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Standards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organic_certification" title="Organic certification">Organic certification</a></div>
<p>Standards regulate production methods and in some cases final output for organic agriculture. Standards may be voluntary or legislated. As early as the 1970s private associations certified organic producers. In the 1980s, governments began to produce organic production guidelines. In the 1990s, a trend toward legislated standards began, most notably with the 1991 <a href="/wiki/EU-Eco-regulation" title="EU-Eco-regulation">EU-Eco-regulation</a> developed for <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> which set standards for 12 countries, and a 1993 UK program. The EU's program was followed by a Japanese program in 2001, and in 2002 the U.S. created the <a href="/wiki/National_Organic_Program" title="National Organic Program">National Organic Program</a> (NOP).<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> As of 2007 over 60 countries regulate organic farming (<a href="#refWorldStats2007">IFOAM 2007:11</a>). In 2005 IFOAM created the <a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Organic_Agriculture" title="Principles of Organic Agriculture">Principles of Organic Agriculture</a>, an international guideline for certification criteria.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Typically the agencies accredit certification groups rather than individual farms.</p>
<p>Organic production materials used in and foods are tested independently by the Organic Materials Review Institute.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Composting">Composting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Composting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Using manure as a fertiliser risks contaminating food with animal gut bacteria, including pathogenic strains of E. coli that have caused fatal poisoning from eating organic food.<sup id="cite_ref-newdaily_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newdaily-85">[85]</a></sup> To combat this risk, <a href="/wiki/USDA" class="mw-redirect" title="USDA">USDA</a> organic standards require that manure must be sterilized through high temperature <a href="/wiki/Composting#Approaches" class="mw-redirect" title="Composting">thermophilic composting</a>. If raw animal manure is used, 120 days must pass before the crop is harvested if the final product comes into direct contact with the soil. For products that don't directly contact soil, 90 days must pass prior to harvest.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a> of organic farming, a subfield of <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_economics" title="Agricultural economics">agricultural economics</a>, encompasses the entire process and effects of organic farming in terms of human society, including <a href="/wiki/Social_cost" title="Social cost">social costs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Opportunity_cost" title="Opportunity cost">opportunity costs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unintended_consequence" class="mw-redirect" title="Unintended consequence">unintended consequences</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information_asymmetries" class="mw-redirect" title="Information asymmetries">information asymmetries</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Economies_of_scale" title="Economies of scale">economies of scale</a>. Although the scope of economics is broad, agricultural economics tends to focus on maximizing yields and efficiency at the farm level. Economics takes an <a href="/wiki/Anthropocentric" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthropocentric">anthropocentric</a> approach to the value of the natural world: biodiversity, for example, is considered beneficial only to the extent that it is valued by people and increases profits. Some entities such as the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> <a href="/wiki/Subsidy" title="Subsidy">subsidize</a> organic farming, in large part because these countries want to account for the <a href="/wiki/Externalities" class="mw-redirect" title="Externalities">externalities</a> of reduced water use, reduced water contamination, reduced <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">soil erosion</a>, reduced carbon emissions, increased biodiversity, and assorted other benefits that result from organic farming.<sup id="cite_ref-Misconcept_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Misconcept-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Traditional organic farming is labor and knowledge-intensive whereas conventional farming is capital-intensive, requiring more energy and manufactured inputs.<sup id="cite_ref-Halberg_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halberg-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Organic farmers in California have cited marketing as their greatest obstacle.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_producer_distribution">Geographic producer distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Geographic producer distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The markets for organic products are strongest in North America and Europe, which as of 2001 are estimated to have $6 and $8 billion respectively of the $20 billion global market.<sup id="cite_ref-Lotter2003_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lotter2003-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:6</sup> As of 2007 <a href="/wiki/Australasia" title="Australasia">Australasia</a> has 39% of the total organic farmland, including Australia's 1,180,000 hectares (2,900,000 acres) but 97 percent of this land is sprawling <a href="/wiki/Rangeland" title="Rangeland">rangeland</a> (<a href="#refWorldStats2007">2007:35</a>). US sales are 20x as much.<sup id="cite_ref-Lotter2003_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lotter2003-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:7</sup> Europe farms 23 percent of global organic farmland (6,900,000&#160;ha (17,000,000 acres)), followed by Latin America with 19 percent (5.8 million hectares - 14.3 million acres). Asia has 9.5 percent while North America has 7.2 percent. Africa has 3 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Besides Australia,<sup id="cite_ref-Atlas_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atlas-1">[1]</a></sup> the countries with the most organic farmland are Argentina (3.1 million hectares - 7.7 million acres), China (2.3 million hectares - 5.7 million acres), and the United States (1.6 million hectares - 4 million acres). Much of Argentina's organic farmland is pasture, like that of Australia (<a href="#refWorldStats2007">2007:42</a>). Spain, Germany, Brazil (the world's largest agricultural exporter), Uruguay, and the UK follow the United States in the amount of organic land (<a href="#refWorldStats2007">2007:26</a>).</p>
<p>In the European Union (<a href="/wiki/EU25#2004_Enlargement" class="mw-redirect" title="EU25">EU25</a>) 3.9% of the total utilized agricultural area was used for organic production in 2005. The countries with the highest proportion of organic land were Austria (11%) and Italy (8.4%), followed by the Czech Republic and Greece (both 7.2%). The lowest figures were shown for Malta (0.1%), Poland (0.6%) and Ireland (0.8%).<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> In 2009, the proportion of organic land in the EU grew to 4.7%. The countries with highest share of agricultural land were Liechtenstein (26.9%), Austria (18.5%) and Sweden (12.6%).<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> 16% of all farmers in Austria produced organically in 2010. By the same year the proportion of organic land increased to 20%.:<sup id="cite_ref-BZ_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BZ-93">[93]</a></sup> In 2005 168,000 ha (415,000 ac) of land in Poland was under organic management.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> In 2012, 288,261 hectares (712,308 acres) were under organic production, and there were about 15,500 organic farmers; retail sales of organic products were EUR 80 million in 2011. As of 2012 organic exports were part of the government's economic development strategy.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> in 1991, agricultural inputs that had previously been purchased from <a href="/wiki/Eastern_bloc" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern bloc">Eastern bloc</a> countries were no longer available in Cuba, and many Cuban farms converted to organic methods out of necessity.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Consequently, organic agriculture is a mainstream practice in Cuba, while it remains an alternative practice in most other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-Commission_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Commission-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Cuba's organic strategy includes development of <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_crops" title="Genetically modified crops">genetically modified crops</a>; specifically corn that is resistant to the palomilla moth<sup id="cite_ref-Commission_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Commission-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth">Growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Growth_of_organic_farmland_since_2000.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Growth_of_organic_farmland_since_2000.png/220px-Growth_of_organic_farmland_since_2000.png" width="220" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Growth_of_organic_farmland_since_2000.png/330px-Growth_of_organic_farmland_since_2000.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d6/Growth_of_organic_farmland_since_2000.png 2x" data-file-width="356" data-file-height="318" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Growth_of_organic_farmland_since_2000.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Organic farmland by world region (2000-2008)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2001, the global market value of certified organic products was estimated at USD $20 billion. By 2002, this was USD $23 billion and by 2015 more than USD $43 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-OrgWorldYearbook2011_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OrgWorldYearbook2011-99">[99]</a></sup> By 2014, retail sales of organic products reached USD $80 billion worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-OrgWorldYearbook2016_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OrgWorldYearbook2016-100">[100]</a></sup> North America and Europe accounted for more than 90% of all organic product sales.<sup id="cite_ref-OrgWorldYearbook2016_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OrgWorldYearbook2016-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>Organic agricultural land increased almost fourfold in 15 years, from 11 million hectares in 1999 to 43.7 million hectares in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-OrgWorldYearbook2016_100-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OrgWorldYearbook2016-100">[100]</a></sup> Between 2013 and 2014, organic agricultural land grew by 500,000 hectares worldwide, increasing in every region except Latin America.<sup id="cite_ref-OrgWorldYearbook2016_100-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OrgWorldYearbook2016-100">[100]</a></sup> During this time period, Europe’s organic farmland increased 260,000 hectares to 11.6 million total (+2.3%), Asia’s increased 159,000 hectares to 3.6 million total (+4.7%), Africa’s increased 54,000 hectares to 1.3 million total (+4.5%), and North America’s increased 35,000 hectares to 3.1 million total (+1.1%).<sup id="cite_ref-OrgWorldYearbook2016_100-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OrgWorldYearbook2016-100">[100]</a></sup> As of 2014, the country with the most organic land was Australia (17.2 million hectares), followed by Argentina (3.1 million hectares), and the United States (2.2 million hectares).<sup id="cite_ref-OrgWorldYearbook2016_100-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OrgWorldYearbook2016-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2013, the number of organic producers grew by almost 270,000, or more than 13%.<sup id="cite_ref-OrgWorldYearbook2016_100-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OrgWorldYearbook2016-100">[100]</a></sup> By 2014, there were a reported 2.3 million organic producers in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-OrgWorldYearbook2016_100-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OrgWorldYearbook2016-100">[100]</a></sup> Most of the total global increase took place in the Philippines, Peru, China, and Thailand.<sup id="cite_ref-OrgWorldYearbook2016_100-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OrgWorldYearbook2016-100">[100]</a></sup> Overall, the majority of all organic producers are in India (650,000 in 2013), Uganda (190,552 in 2014), Mexico (169,703 in 2013) and the Philippines (165,974 in 2014).<sup id="cite_ref-OrgWorldYearbook2016_100-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OrgWorldYearbook2016-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Productivity">Productivity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Productivity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Studies comparing yields have had mixed results.<sup id="cite_ref-Welsh.2C_Rick_1999_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welsh.2C_Rick_1999-101">[101]</a></sup> These differences among findings can often be attributed to variations between study designs including differences in the crops studied and the methodology by which results were gathered.</p>
<p>A 2012 meta-analysis found that productivity is typically lower for organic farming than conventional farming, but that the size of the difference depends on context and in some cases may be very small.<sup id="cite_ref-Verena2013_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Verena2013-102">[102]</a></sup> While organic yields can be lower than conventional yields, another meta-analysis published in Sustainable Agriculture Research in 2015, concluded that certain organic on-farm practices could help narrow this gap. Timely weed management and the application of manure in conjunction with legume forages/cover crops were shown to have positive results in increasing organic corn and soybean productivity. More experienced organic farmers were also found to have higher yields than other organic farmers who were just starting out.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another meta-analysis published in the journal Agricultural Systems in 2011 analyzed 362 datasets and found that organic yields were on average 80% of conventional yields. The author's found that there are relative differences in this yield gap based on crop type with crops like soybeans and rice scoring higher than the 80% average and crops like wheat and potato scoring lower. Across global regions, Asia and Central Europe were found to have relatively higher yields and Northern Europe relatively lower than the average.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2007 study<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> compiling research from 293 different comparisons into a single study to assess the overall efficiency of the two agricultural systems has concluded that "organic methods could produce enough food on a global per capita basis to sustain the current human population, and potentially an even larger population, without increasing the agricultural land base." The researchers also found that while in developed countries, organic systems on average produce 92% of the yield produced by conventional agriculture, organic systems produce 80% more than conventional farms in developing countries, because the materials needed for organic farming are more accessible than synthetic farming materials to farmers in some poor countries. This study was strongly contested by another study published in 2008, which stated, and was entitled, "Organic agriculture cannot feed the world"<sup id="cite_ref-Connor2008_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Connor2008-106">[106]</a></sup> and said that the 2007 came up with "a major overestimation of the productivity of OA" "because data are misinterpreted and calculations accordingly are erroneous." Additional research needs to be conducted in the future to further clarify these claims.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Long_term_studies">Long term studies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Long term studies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A study published in 2005 compared conventional cropping, organic animal-based cropping, and organic legume-based cropping on a test farm at the <a href="/wiki/Rodale_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="Rodale Institute">Rodale Institute</a> over 22 years.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The study found that "the crop yields for corn and soybeans were similar in the organic animal, organic legume, and conventional farming systems". It also found that "significantly less fossil energy was expended to produce corn in the Rodale Institute’s organic animal and organic legume systems than in the conventional production system. There was little difference in energy input between the different treatments for producing soybeans. In the organic systems, synthetic fertilizers and pesticides were generally not used". As of 2013 the Rodale study was ongoing<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> and a thirty-year anniversary report was published by Rodale in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>A long-term field study comparing organic/conventional agriculture carried out over 21 years in Switzerland concluded that "Crop yields of the organic systems averaged over 21 experimental years at 80% of the conventional ones. The fertilizer input, however, was 34 – 51% lower, indicating an efficient production. The organic farming systems used 20 – 56% less energy to produce a crop unit and per land area this difference was 36 – 53%. In spite of the considerably lower pesticide input the quality of organic products was hardly discernible from conventional analytically and even came off better in food preference trials and picture creating methods"<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Profitability">Profitability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Profitability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the United States, organic farming has been shown to be 2.9 to 3.8 times more profitable for the farmer than conventional farming when prevailing price premiums are taken into account.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Globally, organic farming is between 22 and 35 percent more profitable for farmers than conventional methods, according to a 2015 meta-analysis of studies conducted across five continents.<sup id="cite_ref-Crowder_7611.E2.80.937616_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crowder_7611.E2.80.937616-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>The profitability of organic agriculture can be attributed to a number of factors. First, organic farmers do not rely on synthetic fertilizer and pesticide inputs, which can be costly. In addition, organic foods currently enjoy a price premium over conventionally produced foods, meaning that organic farmers can often get more for their yield.</p>
<p>The price premium for organic food is an important factor in the economic viability of organic farming. In 2013 there was a 100% price premium on organic vegetables and a 57% price premium for organic fruits. These percentages are based on wholesale fruit and vegetable prices, available through the United States Department of Agriculture’s Economic Research Service.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Price premiums exist not only for organic versus nonorganic crops, but may also vary depending on the venue where the product is sold: farmers markets, grocery stores, or wholesale to restaurants. For many producers, direct sales at farmers markets are most profitable because the farmer receives the entire markup, however this is also the most time and labor-intensive approach.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>There have been signs of organic price premiums narrowing in recent years, which lowers the economic incentive for farmers to convert to or maintain organic production methods.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-115">[115]</a></sup> Data from 22 years of experiments at the Rodale Institute found that, based on the current yields and production costs associated with organic farming in the United States, a price premium of only 10% is required to achieve parity with conventional farming.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-115">[115]</a></sup> A separate study found that on a global scale, price premiums of only 5-7% percent were needed to break even with conventional methods.<sup id="cite_ref-Crowder_7611.E2.80.937616_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crowder_7611.E2.80.937616-112">[112]</a></sup> Without the price premium, profitability for farmers is mixed.<sup id="cite_ref-Lotter2003_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lotter2003-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:11</sup></p>
