<p>For markets and supermarkets organic food is profitable as well, and is generally sold at significantly higher prices than non-organic food.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_efficiency">Energy efficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Energy efficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the most recent assessments of the energy efficiency of organic versus conventional agriculture, results have been mixed regarding which form is more carbon efficient. Organic farm systems have more often than not been found to be more energy efficient, however, this is not always the case. More than anything, results tend to depend upon crop type and farm size.<sup id="cite_ref-Pimental_1983_359.E2.80.93372_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pimental_1983_359.E2.80.93372-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>A comprehensive comparison of energy efficiency in grain production, produce yield, and animal husbandry concluded that organic farming had a higher yield per unit of energy over the vast majority of the crops and livestock systems.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> For example, two studies - both comparing organically- versus conventionally-farmed apples - declare contradicting results, one saying organic farming is more energy efficient, the other saying conventionally is more efficient.<sup id="cite_ref-Pimental_1983_359.E2.80.93372_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pimental_1983_359.E2.80.93372-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reganold_et_al._2001_926.E2.80.93930_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reganold_et_al._2001_926.E2.80.93930-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>It has generally been found that the labor input per unit of yield was higher for organic systems compared with conventional production.<sup id="cite_ref-Pimental_1983_359.E2.80.93372_117-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pimental_1983_359.E2.80.93372-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sales_and_marketing">Sales and marketing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Sales and marketing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most sales are concentrated in developed nations. In 2008, 69% of Americans claimed to occasionally buy organic products, down from 73% in 2005. One theory for this change was that consumers were substituting "local" produce for "organic" produce.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distributors">Distributors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Distributors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">USDA</a> requires that distributors, manufacturers, and processors of organic products be certified by an accredited state or private agency.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-122">[122]</a></sup> In 2007, there were 3,225 certified organic handlers, up from 2,790 in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-ers.usda.gov_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ers.usda.gov-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>Organic handlers are often small firms; 48% reported sales below $1 million annually, and 22% between $1 and $5 million per year.<sup id="cite_ref-Oberholtzer_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oberholtzer-124">[124]</a></sup> Smaller handlers are more likely to sell to independent natural grocery stores and natural product chains whereas large distributors more often market to natural product chains and conventional supermarkets, with a small group marketing to independent natural product stores.<sup id="cite_ref-ers.usda.gov_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ers.usda.gov-123">[123]</a></sup> Some handlers work with conventional farmers to convert their land to organic with the knowledge that the farmer will have a secure sales outlet. This lowers the risk for the handler as well as the farmer. In 2004, 31% of handlers provided technical support on organic standards or production to their suppliers and 34% encouraged their suppliers to transition to organic.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-122">[122]</a></sup> Smaller farms often join together in <a href="/wiki/Cooperative" title="Cooperative">cooperatives</a> to market their goods more effectively.</p>
<p>93% of organic sales are through conventional and natural food supermarkets and chains, while the remaining 7% of U.S. organic food sales occur through farmers' markets, <a href="/wiki/Foodservice" title="Foodservice">foodservices</a>, and other marketing channels.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Direct-to-Consumer_Sales">Direct-to-Consumer Sales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Direct-to-Consumer Sales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the 2012 Census, direct-to-consumer sales equaled $1.3 billion, up from $812 million in 2002, an increase of 60 percent. The number of farms that utilize direct-to-consumer sales was 144,530 in 2012 in comparison to 116,733 in 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Direct-to-consumer sales include farmers markets, <a href="/wiki/Community-supported_agriculture" title="Community-supported agriculture">community supported agriculture</a> (CSA), on-farm stores, and roadside farm stands. Some organic farms also sell products direct to retailer, direct to restaurant and direct to institution.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> According to the 2008 Organic Production Survey, approximately 7% of organic farm sales went direct-to-consumers, 10% went direct to retailers, and approximately 83% went into wholesale markets. In comparison, only 0.4% of the value of convention agricultural commodities went direct-to-consumers.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>While not all products sold at farmer’s markets are certified organic, this direct-to-consumer avenue has become increasingly popular in local food distribution and has grown substantially since 1994. In 2014, there were 8,284 farmer’s markets in comparison to 3,706 in 2004 and 1,755 in 1994, most of which are found in populated areas such as the Northeast, Midwest, and West Coast.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Labor_and_employment">Labor and employment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Labor and employment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Organic production is more labor-intensive than conventional production.<sup id="cite_ref-FAOFAQ_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAOFAQ-130">[130]</a></sup> On the one hand, this increased labor cost is one factor that makes organic food more expensive.<sup id="cite_ref-FAOFAQ_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAOFAQ-130">[130]</a></sup> On the other hand, the increased need for labor may be seen as an "employment dividend" of organic farming, providing more jobs per unit area than conventional systems.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> The 2011 UNEP Green Economy Report suggests that "[a]n increase in investment in green agriculture is projected to lead to growth in employment of about 60 per cent compared with current levels" and that "green agriculture investments could create 47 million additional jobs compared with BAU2 over the next 40 years."<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> The UNEP also argues that "[b]y greening agriculture and food distribution, more calories per person per day, more jobs and business opportunities especially in rural areas, and market-access opportunities, especially for developing countries, will be available."</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World.27s_food_security">World's food security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: World's food security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2007 the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> (FAO) said that organic agriculture often leads to higher prices and hence a better income for farmers, so it should be promoted. However, FAO stressed that by organic farming one could not feed the current mankind, even less the bigger future population. Both data and models showed then that organic farming was far from sufficient. Therefore, chemical fertilizers were needed to avoid hunger.<sup id="cite_ref-fao_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fao-133">[133]</a></sup> Other analysis by many agribusiness executives, agricultural and ecological scientists, and international agriculture experts revealed the opinion that organic farming would not only increase the world's food supply, but might be the only way to eradicate hunger.<sup id="cite_ref-Halweil_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halweil-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>FAO stressed that fertilizers and other chemical inputs can much increase the production, particularly in Africa where fertilizers are currently used 90% less than in Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-fao_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fao-133">[133]</a></sup> For example, in Malawi the yield has been boosted using seeds and fertilizers.<sup id="cite_ref-fao_133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fao-133">[133]</a></sup> FAO also calls for using <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a>, as it can help smallholder farmers to improve their income and food security.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also <a href="/wiki/NEPAD" class="mw-redirect" title="NEPAD">NEPAD</a>, development organization of African governments, announced that feeding Africans and preventing malnutrition requires fertilizers and enhanced seeds.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a more recent study in ScienceDigest, organic best management practices shows an average yield only 13% less than conventional.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> In the world's poorer nations where most of the world's hungry live, and where conventional agriculture's expensive inputs are not affordable by the majority of farmers, adopting organic management actually increases yields 93% on average, and could be an important part of increased food security.<sup id="cite_ref-Halweil_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halweil-134">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Capacity_building_in_developing_countries">Capacity building in developing countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Capacity building in developing countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Organic agriculture can contribute to ecologically sustainable, socio-economic development, especially in poorer countries.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> The application of organic principles enables employment of local resources (e.g., local seed varieties, manure, etc.) and therefore cost-effectiveness. Local and international markets for organic products show tremendous growth prospects and offer creative producers and exporters excellent opportunities to improve their income and living conditions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Organic agriculture is knowledge intensive. Globally, capacity building efforts are underway, including localized training material, to limited effect. As of 2007, the <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_of_Organic_Agriculture_Movements" class="mw-redirect" title="International Federation of Organic Agriculture Movements">International Federation of Organic Agriculture Movements</a> hosted more than 170 free manuals and 75 training opportunities online.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 2008 the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environmental_Programme" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations Environmental Programme">United Nations Environmental Programme</a> (UNEP) and the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Conference_on_Trade_and_Development" title="United Nations Conference on Trade and Development">United Nations Conference on Trade and Development</a> (UNCTAD) stated that "organic agriculture can be more conducive to food security in Africa than most conventional production systems, and that it is more likely to be sustainable in the long-term"<sup id="cite_ref-UNEP2008_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP2008-140">[140]</a></sup> and that "yields had more than doubled where organic, or near-organic practices had been used" and that soil fertility and drought resistance improved.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Millennium_Development_Goals">Millennium Development Goals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Millennium Development Goals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The value of organic agriculture (OA) in the achievement of the <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Development_Goals" title="Millennium Development Goals">Millennium Development Goals</a> (MDG), particularly in poverty reduction efforts in the face of climate change, is shown by its contribution to both income and non-income aspects of the MDGs. These benefits are expected to continue in the post-MDG era. A series of case studies conducted in selected areas in Asian countries by the Asian Development Bank Institute (ADBI) and published as a book compilation by ADB in Manila document these contributions to both income and non-income aspects of the MDGs. These include poverty alleviation by way of higher incomes, improved farmers' health owing to less chemical exposure, integration of sustainable principles into rural development policies, improvement of access to safe water and sanitation, and expansion of global partnership for development as small farmers are integrated in value chains.<sup id="cite_ref-E.26S_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-E.26S-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>A related ADBI study also sheds on the costs of OA programs and set them in the context of the costs of attaining the MDGs. The results show considerable variation across the case studies, suggesting that there is no clear structure to the costs of adopting OA. Costs depend on the efficiency of the OA adoption programs. The lowest cost programs were more than ten times less expensive than the highest cost ones. However, further analysis of the gains resulting from OA adoption reveals that the costs per person taken out of poverty was much lower than the estimates of the World Bank,<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-143">[143]</a></sup> based on income growth in general or based on the detailed costs of meeting some of the more quantifiable MDGs (e.g., education, health, and environment).<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Disadvantages">Disadvantages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Disadvantages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Agriculture imposes negative externalities (uncompensated costs) upon society through public land and other public resource use, biodiversity loss, erosion, pesticides, nutrient runoff, subsidized water usage, subsidy payments and assorted other problems. Positive externalities include self-reliance, entrepreneurship, respect for nature, and air quality. Organic methods reduce some of these costs.<sup id="cite_ref-Marshall1991_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall1991-145">[145]</a></sup> In 2000 uncompensated costs for 1996 reached 2,343 million British pounds or £208 per ha (£84.20/ac).<sup id="cite_ref-Pretty2000_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pretty2000-146">[146]</a></sup> A study of practices in the USA published in 2005 concluded that cropland costs the economy approximately 5 to 16 billion dollars ($30–96/ha - $12–39/ac), while livestock production costs 714 million dollars.<sup id="cite_ref-Tegtmeier2005_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tegtmeier2005-147">[147]</a></sup> Both studies recommended reducing externalities. The 2000 review included reported pesticide poisonings but did not include speculative chronic health effects of pesticides, and the 2004 review relied on a 1992 estimate of the total impact of pesticides.</p>
<p>It has been proposed that organic agriculture can reduce the level of some negative externalities from (conventional) agriculture. Whether the benefits are private or public depends upon the division of property rights.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several surveys and studies have attempted to examine and compare conventional and organic systems of farming and have found that organic techniques, while not without harm, are less damaging than conventional ones because they reduce levels of biodiversity less than conventional systems do and use less energy and produce less waste when calculated per unit area.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2003 to 2005 investigation by the Cranfield University for the Department for Environment Food and Rural Affairs in the UK found that it is difficult to compare the Global Warming Potential (GWP), acidification and eutrophication emissions but "Organic production often results in increased burdens, from factors such as N leaching and N2O emissions", even though primary energy use was less for most organic products. N<sub>2</sub>O is always the largest GWP contributor except in tomatoes. However, "organic tomatoes always incur more burdens (except pesticide use)". Some emissions were lower "per area", but organic farming always required 65 to 200% more field area than non-organic farming. The numbers were highest for bread wheat (200+&#160;% more) and potatoes (160% more).<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>The situation was shown dramatically in a comparison of a modern dairy farm in Wisconsin with one in New Zealand in which the animals grazed extensively.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-153">[153]</a></sup> Using total farm emissions per kg milk produced as a parameter, the researchers showed that production of methane from belching was higher in the New Zealand farm, while carbon dioxide production was higher in the Wisconsin farm. Output of nitrous oxide, a gas with an estimated global warming potential 310 times that of carbon dioxide was also higher in the New Zealand farm. Methane from manure handling was similar in the two types of farm. The explanation for the finding relates to the different diets used on these farms, being based more completely on forage (and hence more fibrous) in New Zealand and containing less concentrate than in Wisconsin. Fibrous diets promote a higher proportion of acetate in the gut of ruminant animals, resulting in a higher production of methane that must be released by belching. When cattle are given a diet containing some concentrates (such as corn and soybean meal) in addition to grass and silage, the pattern of ruminal fermentation alters from acetate to mainly propionate. As a result, methane production is reduced. Capper et al. compared the environmental impact of US dairy production in 1944 and 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-Capper_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Capper-154">[154]</a></sup> They calculated that the carbon “footprint” per billion kg (2.2 billion lb) of milk produced in 2007 was 37 percent that of equivalent milk production in 1944.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact_and_emissions">Environmental impact and emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Environmental impact and emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Researchers at Oxford university analyzed 71 peer-reviewed studies and observed that organic products are sometimes worse for the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-oxford_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-155">[155]</a></sup> Organic milk, cereals, and pork generated higher <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions per product than conventional ones but organic beef and olives had lower emissions in most studies.<sup id="cite_ref-oxford_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-155">[155]</a></sup> Usually organic products required less energy, but more land.<sup id="cite_ref-oxford_155-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-155">[155]</a></sup> Per unit of product, organic produce generates higher nitrogen leaching, nitrous oxide emissions, ammonia emissions, <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a> and acidification potential than when conventionally grown.<sup id="cite_ref-tuomisto_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tuomisto-156">[156]</a></sup> Other differences were not significant.<sup id="cite_ref-tuomisto_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tuomisto-156">[156]</a></sup> The researchers concluded, as there is not singular way of doing conventional or organic farming, that the debate should go beyond the conventional vs organic debate, and more about finding specific solutions to specific circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-tuomisto_156-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tuomisto-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>Proponents of organic farming have claimed that organic agriculture emphasizes closed nutrient cycles, biodiversity, and effective soil management providing the capacity to mitigate and even reverse the effects of <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> and that organic agriculture can decrease fossil fuel emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-Rodale2014_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rodale2014-158">[158]</a></sup> "The carbon sequestration efficiency of organic systems in temperate climates is almost double (575-700 kg carbon per ha per year - 510-625 lb/ac/an ) that of conventional treatment of soils, mainly owing to the use of grass clovers for feed and of cover crops in organic rotations."<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critics of organic farming methods believe that the increased land needed to farm organic food could potentially destroy the rainforests and wipe out many ecosystems.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrient_leaching">Nutrient leaching</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Nutrient leaching">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">meta-analysis</a> of 71 studies, nitrogen leaching, nitrous oxide emissions, ammonia emissions, eutrophication potential and acidification potential were higher for organic products,<sup id="cite_ref-tuomisto_156-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tuomisto-156">[156]</a></sup> although in one study "nitrate leaching was 4.4-5.6 times higher in conventional plots than organic plots".<sup id="cite_ref-PNAS_2006-3-21_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS_2006-3-21-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>Excess nutrients in lakes, rivers, and groundwater can cause <a href="/wiki/Algal_blooms" class="mw-redirect" title="Algal blooms">algal blooms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a>, and subsequent <a href="/wiki/Dead_zone_(ecology)" title="Dead zone (ecology)">dead zones</a>. In addition, <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrates</a> are harmful to aquatic organisms by themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-Science_2001-04-13_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Science_2001-04-13-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_use">Land use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Land use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Oxford meta-analysis of 71 studies found that organic farming requires 84% more land for an equivalent amount of harvest, mainly due to lack of nutrients but sometimes due to weeds, diseases or pests, lower yielding animals and land required for fertility building crops.<sup id="cite_ref-tuomisto_156-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tuomisto-156">[156]</a></sup> While organic farming does not necessarily save land for wildlife habitats and forestry in all cases,<sup id="cite_ref-oxford_155-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-155">[155]</a></sup> the most modern breakthroughs in organic are addressing these issues with success.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>Professor Wolfgang Branscheid says that organic animal production is not good for the environment, because organic chicken requires doubly as much land as "conventional" chicken and organic pork a quarter more.<sup id="cite_ref-wn_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wn-167">[167]</a></sup> According to a calculation by Hudson Institute, organic beef requires triply as much land.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> On the other hand, certain organic methods of animal husbandry have been shown to restore desertified, marginal, and/or otherwise unavailable land to agricultural productivity and wildlife.<sup id="cite_ref-greenbiz_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenbiz-169">[169]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> Or by getting both forage and cash crop production from the same fields simultaneously, reduce net land use.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>In England organic farming yields 55% of normal yields.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> While in other regions of the world, organic methods have started producing record yields.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pesticides">Pesticides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Pesticides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<tr>

<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Organic_Apples_Pateros_WA_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Organic_Apples_Pateros_WA_cropped.jpg/220px-Organic_Apples_Pateros_WA_cropped.jpg" width="220" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Organic_Apples_Pateros_WA_cropped.jpg/330px-Organic_Apples_Pateros_WA_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Organic_Apples_Pateros_WA_cropped.jpg/440px-Organic_Apples_Pateros_WA_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="838" data-file-height="759" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Organic_Apples_Pateros_WA_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A sign outside of an organic <a href="/wiki/Apple" title="Apple">apple</a> orchard in <a href="/wiki/Pateros,_Washington" title="Pateros, Washington">Pateros, Washington</a> reminding orchardists not to spray pesticides on these trees.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In organic farming synthetic pesticides are generally prohibited. A chemical is said to be synthetic if it does not already exist in the natural world. But the organic label goes further and usually prohibit compounds that exist in nature if they are produced by <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">Chemical synthesis</a>. So the prohibition is also about the method of production and not only the nature of the compound.</p>
<p>An non exhaustive list of organic approved pesticides with theirs <a href="/wiki/Median_lethal_dose" title="Median lethal dose">Median lethal dose</a></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_sulfate" title="Copper(II) sulfate">Copper(II) sulfate</a> is used as a fungicide and is also used in conventional agriculture (<a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_sulfate" title="Copper(II) sulfate">LD<sub>50</sub> 300 mg/kg</a>). Conventional agriculture has the option to use the less toxic <a href="/wiki/Mancozeb" title="Mancozeb">Mancozeb</a> (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://pmep.cce.cornell.edu/profiles/extoxnet/haloxyfop-methylparathion/mancozeb-ext.html">LD<sub>50</sub> 4,500 to 11,200 mg/kg</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Boric_acid" title="Boric acid">Boric acid</a> is used as stomach poison that target insects (LD<sub>50</sub>: 2660&#160;mg/kg).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pyrethrin" title="Pyrethrin">Pyrethrin</a> comes from chemicals extracted from flowers of the genus <a href="/wiki/Pyrethrum" title="Pyrethrum">Pyrethrum</a> (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://pmep.cce.cornell.edu/profiles/extoxnet/pyrethrins-ziram/pyrethrins-ext.html">LD<sub>50</sub> of 370 mg/kg</a>). Its potent toxicity is used to control insects.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lime_sulphur" class="mw-redirect" title="Lime sulphur">Lime sulphur</a> (aka calcium polysulfide) and sulfur are considered to be allowed, synthetic materials<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> (LD<sub>50</sub>: 820&#160;mg/kg)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rotenone" title="Rotenone">Rotenone</a> is a powerful insecticide that was used to control insects (LD<sub>50</sub>: 132&#160;mg/kg). Despite the high toxicity of Rotenone to aquatic life and some links to Parkinson disease the compound is still allowed in organic farming as it is a naturally occurring compound.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bromomethane" title="Bromomethane">Bromomethane</a> is a gas that is still used in the nurseries of Strawberry organic farming<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Azadirachtin" title="Azadirachtin">Azadirachtin</a> is a wide spectrum very potent insecticide. Almost non toxic to mammals (LD<sub>50</sub> in rats is &gt; 3,540&#160;mg/kg) but affects beneficial insects.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_quality_and_safety">Food quality and safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Food quality and safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organic_food" title="Organic food">Organic food</a></div>
<p>While there may be some differences in the amounts of nutrients and anti-nutrients when organically produced food and conventionally produced food are compared, the variable nature of food production and handling makes it difficult to generalize results, and there is insufficient evidence to make claims that organic food is safer or healthier than conventional food.<sup id="cite_ref-2014meta_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2014meta-179">[179]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Blair1_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blair1-180">[180]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MagkosSafety2006_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MagkosSafety2006-181">[181]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smith-Spangler2012_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-Spangler2012-182">[182]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FSA_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FSA-183">[183]</a></sup> Claims that organic food tastes better are not supported by evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-Blair1_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blair1-180">[180]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bourn_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bourn-184">[184]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soil_conservation">Soil conservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Soil conservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Soil_conservation" title="Soil conservation">Soil conservation</a></div>
<p>Supporters claim that organically managed soil has a higher quality<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> and higher water retention.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> This may help increase yields for organic farms in drought years. Organic farming can build up soil organic matter better than conventional no-till farming, which suggests long-term yield benefits from organic farming.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> An 18-year study of organic methods on nutrient-depleted soil concluded that conventional methods were superior for soil fertility and yield for nutrient-depleted soils in cold-temperate climates, arguing that much of the benefit from organic farming derives from imported materials that could not be regarded as self-sustaining.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <i>Dirt: The Erosion of Civilizations</i>, geomorphologist David Montgomery outlines a coming crisis from soil <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a>. Agriculture relies on roughly one meter of topsoil, and that is being depleted ten times faster than it is being replaced.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/No-till" class="mw-redirect" title="No-till">No-till</a> farming, which some claim depends upon pesticides, is one way to minimize erosion. However, a 2007 study by the USDA's Agricultural Research Service has found that manure applications in tilled organic farming are better at building up the soil than no-till.<sup id="cite_ref-organicsoil_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-organicsoil-190">[190]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Biodiversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organic_farming_and_biodiversity" title="Organic farming and biodiversity">Organic farming and biodiversity</a></div>
<p>The conservation of natural resources and biodiversity is a core principle of organic production. Three broad management practices (prohibition/reduced use of chemical pesticides and inorganic fertilizers; sympathetic management of non-cropped habitats; and preservation of mixed farming) that are largely intrinsic (but not exclusive) to organic farming are particularly beneficial for farmland wildlife.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-192">[192]</a></sup> Using practices that attract or introduce beneficial insects, provide habitat for birds and mammals, and provide conditions that increase soil biotic diversity serve to supply vital ecological services to organic production systems. Advantages to certified organic operations that implement these types of production practices include: 1) decreased dependence on outside fertility inputs; 2) reduced pest management costs; 3) more reliable sources of clean water; and 4) better pollination.<sup id="cite_ref-Agricultural_Marketing_Service_2016_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Agricultural_Marketing_Service_2016-193">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nearly all non-crop, naturally occurring<sup id="cite_ref-:0_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-194">[194]</a></sup> species observed in comparative farm land practice studies show a preference for organic farming both by abundance and diversity.<sup id="cite_ref-Hole_et_al._2005_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hole_et_al._2005-195">[195]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gabriel_and_Tscharntke_2006_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gabriel_and_Tscharntke_2006-196">[196]</a></sup> An average of 30% more species inhabit organic farms.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> Birds, butterflies, soil microbes, beetles, earthworms,<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> spiders, vegetation, and mammals are particularly affected. Lack of herbicides and pesticides improve biodiversity fitness and population density.<sup id="cite_ref-Gabriel_and_Tscharntke_2006_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gabriel_and_Tscharntke_2006-196">[196]</a></sup> Many weed species attract beneficial insects that improve soil qualities and forage on weed pests.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> Soil-bound organisms often benefit because of increased bacteria populations due to natural fertilizer such as manure, while experiencing reduced intake of <a href="/wiki/Herbicide" title="Herbicide">herbicides</a> and pesticides.<sup id="cite_ref-Hole_et_al._2005_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hole_et_al._2005-195">[195]</a></sup> Increased biodiversity, especially from beneficial soil microbes and <a href="/wiki/Mycorrhizae" class="mw-redirect" title="Mycorrhizae">mycorrhizae</a> have been proposed as an explanation for the high yields experienced by some organic plots, especially in light of the differences seen in a 21-year comparison of organic and control fields.<sup id="cite_ref-Flie.C3.9Fbach_et_al._2006_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flie.C3.9Fbach_et_al._2006-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Biodiversity from organic farming provides capital to humans. Species found in organic farms enhance sustainability by reducing human input (e.g., fertilizers, pesticides).<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup></p>
<p>The USDA’s Agricultural Marketing Service (AMS) published a <i>Federal Register</i> notice on 15 January 2016, announcing the National Organic Program (NOP) final guidance on Natural Resources and Biodiversity Conservation for Certified Organic Operations. Given the broad scope of natural resources which includes soil, water, wetland, woodland and wildlife, the guidance provides examples of practices that support the underlying conservation principles and demonstrate compliance with USDA organic regulations § 205.200.<sup id="cite_ref-Agricultural_Marketing_Service_2016_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Agricultural_Marketing_Service_2016-193">[193]</a></sup> The final guidance provides organic certifiers and farms with examples of production practices that support conservation principles and comply with the USDA organic regulations, which require operations to maintain or improve natural resources.<sup id="cite_ref-Agricultural_Marketing_Service_2016_193-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Agricultural_Marketing_Service_2016-193">[193]</a></sup> The final guidance also clarifies the role of certified operations (to submit an OSP to a certifier), certifiers (ensure that the OSP describes or lists practices that explain the operator's monitoring plan and practices to support natural resources and biodiversity conservation), and inspectors (onsite inspection) in the implementation and verification of these production practices.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup></p>
<p>A wide range of organisms benefit from organic farming, but it is unclear whether organic methods confer greater benefits than conventional integrated agri-environmental programs.<sup id="cite_ref-Hole_et_al._2005_195-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hole_et_al._2005-195">[195]</a></sup> Organic farming is often presented as a more biodiversity-friendly practice, but the generality of the beneficial effects of organic farming is debated as the effects appear often species- and context-dependent, and current research has highlighted the need to quantify the relative effects of local- and landscape-scale management on farmland biodiversity.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> There are four key issues when comparing the impacts on biodiversity of organic and conventional farming: (1) It remains unclear whether a holistic whole-farm approach (i.e. organic) provides greater benefits to biodiversity than carefully targeted prescriptions applied to relatively small areas of cropped and/or non-cropped habitats within conventional agriculture (i.e. agri-environment schemes); (2) Many comparative studies encounter methodological problems, limiting their ability to draw quantitative conclusions; (3) Our knowledge of the impacts of organic farming in pastoral and upland agriculture is limited; (4) There remains a pressing need for longitudinal, system-level studies in order to address these issues and to fill in the gaps in our knowledge of the impacts of organic farming, before a full appraisal of its potential role in biodiversity conservation in agroecosystems can be made.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-192">[192]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_support_for_organic_farming">Regional support for organic farming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Regional support for organic farming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organic_farming&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In India, states such as Sikkim<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> and Kerala<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> have planned to shift to fully organic cultivation by 2015 and 2016 respectively.</p>
